 Document(page_content='Cryo-electron tomograms capture a wealth of structural information on the molecular constituents of cells \nand tissues. We present DeePiCt (Deep Picker in Context), an open-source deep-learning framework for \nsupervised structure segmentation and macromolecular complex localization in cellular cryo-electron \ntomography. To train and benchmark DeePiCt on experimental data, we comprehensively annotated 20 \ntomograms of Schizosaccharomyces pombe for ribosomes, fatty acid synthases, membranes, nuclear pore \ncomplexes, organelles and cytosol. By comparing our method to state-of-the-art approaches on this dataset, \nwe show its unique ability to identify low-abundance and low-density complexes. We use DeePiCt to study \ncompositionally-distinct subpopulations of cellular ribosomes, with emphasis on their contextual \nassociation with mitochondria and the endoplasmic reticulum. Finally, by applying pre-trained networks to \na HeLa cell dataset, we demonstrate that DeePiCt achieves high-quality predictions in unseen datasets from \ndifferent biological species in a matter of minutes. The comprehensively annotated experimental data and \npre-trained networks are provided for immediate exploitation by the community.  \n \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7642822265625, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.7642822265625, 'height': 186.9459228515625, 'nlines': 13, 'fs': 14.380455603966347, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='Main. \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 111.29999542236328, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 39.29999542236328, 'height': 16.744216918945312, 'nlines': 1, 'fs': 16.744216918945312, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='Introduction \nCryo-Electron Tomography (cryo-ET) produces three-dimensional (3D) snapshots of cellular landscapes at \nmolecular resolution, making it possible to investigate the structural and functional states of \nmacromolecular complexes in their native environment, and to unveil how different macromolecular \npopulations interact with other cellular structures1–8. With improved instrumentation, sample preparation \nprotocols, and automation, high-quality in-cell cryo-ET data are rapidly being generated, opening the \npossibility to conduct high-throughput studies9–12. However, due to the complex and crowded nature of the \nintracellular milieu, together with limitations arising from cryo-ET image acquisition (low signal-to-noise \nratio (SNR) and incomplete angular sampling) data mining of 3D cryo-ET volumes remains a major \nbottleneck13,14. Data mining in terms of reliable identification of a relatively homogenous set of \nmacromolecular complexes constitutes a fundamental prerequisite for structural analysis15,13,14. \n \nA range of available semi-automated methods for segmentation of cellular structures and localization of \nmacromolecular complexes (from here on, particles) in cryo-ET datasets are broadly classified as template-\nbased and template-free approaches16. Traditional Template Matching (TM)17 is a commonly applied \ncomputational approach and is based on a point-wise numerical computation of a similarity coefficient \n(cross-correlation) to a known template of the complex in question. TM is accurate in the localization of \nribosomes and cytoskeletal filaments, but fails at identifying smaller or less dense particles18, and is \ncomputationally intensive. On the other hand, current template-free methods implementing classical image \nprocessing approaches are purposely designed for and thus limited to specific molecular shapes, or to \nspecial configurations in which particles are associated with large cellular structures such as membranes or \nmicrotubules16,19,20. These methods together with TM require manual inspection and are therefore laborious \nand time-consuming. The advent of deep learning methods, and particularly convolutional neural networks \n(CNNs)21–23, has enabled developing more generally applicable and automated approaches to accelerate the \ntasks of segmentation and localization in cryo-ET. The first of such methods was a two-dimensional (2D) \nCNN for semantic segmentation of ribosomes and large structures, such as cellular organelles or \nmembranes24. However, its 2D nature makes it less suitable for particle localization, where probing the 3D \nstructure becomes beneficial. More recently, DeepFinder, a fully supervised method based on the U-Net \narchitecture25 for multi-class semantic segmentation, has positioned itself as the state-of-the-art in \nautomated particle localization in both simulated and real cryo-ET datasets26–29. Remaining limitations \ninclude the identification of less prevalent particles, as well as the interpretation of the obtained predictions \nwithin their cellular context. Furthermore, the field lacks publicly available non-synthetic expert-annotated \ncryo-ET data to train new models and to benchmark the available methods.  \n \nHere we present DeePiCt (Deep Picker in Context), our open-source software which synergizes supervised \nconvolutional networks for segmentation of cellular compartments (organelles or cytosol) and structures \n(membranes or cytoskeletal filaments), and localization of particles. We generated a set of comprehensively \nannotated tomograms of wild-type S. pombe for training and benchmarking our method, which we make \npublicly available to overcome the critical limitations arising from the absence of publicly available \nannotated experimental datasets. From here on, we refer to the term network as the deep learning algorithm \nitself and model as the algorithm once already trained. We provide DeePiCt models, trained on this dataset \nwhich show high data-mining performance and can be readily applied across species and datasets.  \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7666015625, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.7666015625, 'height': 614.1817016601562, 'nlines': 42, 'fs': 14.623373849051339, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='Results \n \nDeePiCt: A deep-learning approach for automated compartment segmentation and particle \nlocalization  \nDeePiCt is based on deep learning technologies. It combines a 2D CNN for segmentation of cellular \ncompartments and a 3D CNN for particle localization, membrane, and cytoskeletal filament annotations \n(Fig. 1). This synergy allows for more precise particle picking and interpretation of their cellular context. \nThe 2D and 3D CNN are adapted from the original U-Net architecture25 (Fig. 1a, Supplementary Note 1). \nSince cellular compartments are easily recognizable in 2D image data, the compartment segmentation \nnetwork operates in 2D, which has the advantage of requiring little training data. The 2D CNN employs a \nfixed depth (D) of 5 (4 max-pooling layers) and 16 initial filters (IF) (Fig. 1a). Our implementation of the \n3D CNN allows multi-label learning (Supplementary Note 1) and adjustable architectural parameters D, \nnumber of IF, a batch normalization layer (BN), and the dropout parameter in the encoder and decoder \npaths (ED and DD, respectively) (Fig. 1a). These parameters can be set according to the amount and quality \nof training data, and the shape complexity, size and abundance of the particle of interest as suggested in \nSupplementary Table 1. In general, larger particles benefit from larger values of D to increase the \nnetwork’s receptive field, and particles with a low-density print (low SNR with respect to the surrounding \ncontext) require larger IF values. The remaining optional layers, BN and dropout, are well known \ntechniques in computer vision to avoid overfitting30,31. \n \nFor training, each network requires tomograms and corresponding 3D binary masks, which represent the \nsegmentation of the structures of interest, e.g. organelle segmentations for the 2D U-Net and spheres \nrepresenting particles for the 3D U-Net (Fig. 1b.1). The raw input tomograms are optionally preprocessed \nusing an amplitude spectrum equalization filter (Fig. 1b.2, Extended Data Fig. 1) to enhance image \ncontrast by matching the amplitude spectrum of the input tomogram to a preselected target amplitude \nspectrum distribution (Extended Data Fig. 1). Both CNNs use the Adam optimizer algorithm and the Dice \nLoss function for training (Supplementary Note 1). In the 2D network, tiles are randomly flipped and \nrotated in 90-degree increments during training to improve generalization. For the 3D CNN, we \nimplemented a data augmentation strategy by applying a number of optional random transformations to the \ninput images (Supplementary Note 1). In our experience, more than 700 annotated instances are required \nfor particle learning in the 3D CNNs, while about 5 tomograms are sufficient for membrane segmentation. \nIn the case of 2D CNNs, 6 fully segmented tomograms are enough for training networks identifying either \na cytosol class or a single class including all cellular organelles. \n \nFor predicting, the trained networks receive as input unseen tomograms pre-processed in the same way as \nthe training data, and output 3D probability maps that are subsequently post-processed (Fig. 1b.3). \nAutomated post-processing for the 2D network includes the combination of predicted 2D slices into a 3D \nvolume, application of a 1D Gaussian filter along the z-axis for smoothing the predictions across the z \nslices, and thresholding (user-definable, default=0.75) (Extended Data Fig. 2a-c). The 3D CNN post-\nprocessing entails the generation of a predicted segmentation. To that end, the 3D CNN output is \nthresholded by a probability value set by the user and then clustered. The clustered output can be integrated \nwith contextual information from a binary map representing a tomographic region (e.g. the cytosol \nsegmentation from the 2D CNN). The mode of integration can be chosen among three different options: \nintersection, contact, or colocalization, depending on the users’ specific application (Fig. 1b.4, \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 543.0001220703125, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 471.0001220703125, 'height': 645.6217041015625, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.673220547762783, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='Supplementary Note 1, Supplementary Fig. 1). In the case of particle localization, the clusters’ centroids \nare used to obtain a list of the particle coordinates; their orientations and structural features can then be \nobtained by subtomogram analysis in external softwares (e. g. Warp32, M33, RELION34, Dynamo35, \nPyTom36 or EMAN237). For more details on the method, we refer to Supplementary Note 1 and our github \nrepository (Code and data availability). \n \nGeneration of comprehensive ground-truth annotations in S. pombe \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7622680664062, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.76226806640625, 'height': 100.75360107421875, 'nlines': 7, 'fs': 14.39337158203125, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='The ultimate requirement for developing and benchmarking any deep neural network is the existence of a \nground truth (gt) non-synthetic dataset. Since this was not available at the outset of this study, we first \ncreated a comprehensive annotation of cellular features in wild-type S. pombe representing diverse \nstructures, sizes and abundances. \n \nIn cryo-ET data, the complete annotation of a given cellular structure or particle species is a challenging \nprocess, even for large compartments or complexes with a clear structural signature like the ribosome. To \nachieve the most comprehensive annotation possible, we devised an iterative workflow to mine patterns in \nthree different datasets (Online Methods). In a set of 10 tomograms acquired by combining defocus and \nVolta potential phase plate (VPP38,39) and a second set of 10 tomograms using defocus only (DEF), we \nannotated ribosomes (RIBO), fatty acid synthases (FAS), membranes, organelles and cytoplasm  (Fig. 2, \nExtended Data Tables 1-3). For the nuclear pore complex (NPC), given its structural flexibility, low \nabundance (on average 3 per tomogram), and large size, a third dataset denoted by DEF* was used40. It \nconsists of 127 tomograms featuring a total of 2,830 NPC subunits (~354 NPCs) to ensure enough training \ndata and is composed of two subsets: one of higher and one of lower quality classified based on lamella \nthickness and tilt-series alignment error. A detailed description of the ground truth construction is available \nin the Online Methods.  \n \nThe resulting ground truth RIBO annotation in VPP consisted of 25,311 particles. An initial TM annotation \ncontributed 61.6% (1,559 particles per tomogram (ppt), on average) of the final ground truth, whereas \niterative rounds of DeePiCt on the incomplete ground truth contributed to a total of additional 21.36% (541 \nppt on average) and final manual annotations to 17.05% (431 ppt on average) (Fig. 2j, Extended Data Fig. \n3a,e, Extended Data Table 2). \n \nThe final ground truth annotation of FAS in the 10 VPP datasets amounts to 731 particles, from which \n58.96% (43 ppt on average) constituted the initial annotations from manual picking, as TM failed. The \ncombined predictions of DeePiCt on the incomplete ground truth contributed to 21.88% (16 ppt on average). \nFor FAS we performed only one round of DeePiCt using the initial manual annotations, since we observed \na high false positive rate in subsequent rounds, which is likely a consequence of the natural lower abundance \nof FAS in the tomograms and its low imprint density. The final manual picking added a significant fraction \nof 19% (14 ppt on average) (Extended Data Fig. 3c,e, Extended Data Table 3). \n \nThe final ground truth RIBO annotations on the DEF dataset sum a total of 25,901 particles across the 10 \ntomograms, from which only 19.23% (498 ppt on average) were included in the initial TM annotation (step \n1), 19.39% (502 ppt on average) were a contribution of DeePiCt on incomplete ground truth, and 61.36% \n(1,590 ppt on average) were manually identified (Fig. 2k-m, Extended Data Fig. 3b,e), highlighting the \nvalue of our carefully curated annotations. In the case of FAS, the ground truth annotations across the 10 \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7744140625, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.7744140625, 'height': 535.9059448242188, 'nlines': 37, 'fs': 14.483944454708615, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content="tomograms amount to 366 particles, from which 48.63% (18 ppt on average) came from the initial manual \nannotations (as TM failed), 36.88% (14 ppt on average) from DeePiCt trained on incomplete ground truth, \nand 14.48% (5 ppt on average) from the final manual picking (Fig. 2k-m, Extended data Fig. 3d,f). \nOverall, a final round of manual picking for RIBO ground truth annotation was notably more crucial in \nDEF compared to VPP.  \n \nTotal numbers of RIBO in both acquisition types are comparable, while for FAS fewer particles were \ndetected in DEF likely due to the lower SNR despite the application of an equalization filter (Extended \nData Fig. 3, Extended Data Table 2-3, Supplementary Note 2). FAS annotations were similarly \ndistributed among the ground truth construction steps in the two acquisition conditions (Extended Data \nFig. 3). Comparison to previous proteomics studies41,42 shows that our annotations of fully assembled FAS \nin cryo-electron tomograms is in agreement with levels of FAS-𝛼 and FAS-𝛽 quantified by means of mass \nspectrometry, while the abundance of cytosolic ribosomal proteins detected by mass spectrometry are \nunderestimated (Extended Data Fig. 4).  \n \nTo assess the quality of the RIBO and FAS particle annotations in the VPP and DEF datasets, we processed \nthe tomograms in Warp and performed structural 3D classification and refinement of the subtomograms in \nRELION (Extended Data Tables 4-5, Supplementary Note. 2, Supplementary Fig. 2-4). Subtomogram \naverages of RIBO particles detected only by DeePiCt ('tp new'), represent 80S ribosomes, consistent with \nmaps of all cytosolic RIBO particles for both VPP and DEF datasets (Fig. 2l-m, blue and gray, \nrespectively). 3D classifications performed in both data types for FAS demonstrated that the 3D CNN \ntogether with manual annotations were capable of recovering these challenging shell-like structures \nindependent of the data acquisition type (Supplementary Note 2, Supplementary Fig. 3).  \n \nIn conclusion, using DeePiCt, we provide comprehensive annotations for large macromolecular complexes \nand cellular structures in S. pombe cryo-electron tomograms acquired with VPP and DEF, which set the \nground for benchmarking the method’s performance and for future developments in particle detection \napproaches. \n \nPerformance analysis and hyperparameter tuning of the DeePiCt workflow \n \nPerformance analysis of 2D CNN in VPP \nFor the 2D CNN, basic hyperparameter tuning had close to no effect and a fixed architecture produced \nsufficiently good segmentations. Hence, no extensive hyper-parameter tuning was performed. The 2D \nCNN's performance was evaluated on 10 manually annotated ground-truth VPP tomograms in a 5-fold \ncross-validation (CV). For each CV fold, two tomograms were split off from the training data to evaluate \nmodel performance during training. Two binary segmentation tasks were evaluated: segmentation of all \norganelles, i.e. all membrane-enclosed organelles and the nucleoplasm, and segmentation of the cytosol. \nThe results show that the 2D CNN achieves high areas under the precision-recall curve (AUPRC, \nSupplementary Note 1), with a median AUPRC of 0.98 for cytosol and 0.92 for organelles (Fig. 3a, \nExtended Data Fig. 5). \n \nThe 2D CNN showed an average false positive rate (FPR) of 0.054 for cytosol and 0.024 for organelle \nsegmentation, with the post-processing step providing marginal improvements (reducing FPR to 0.049 for \n", metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7688598632812, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.76885986328125, 'height': 639.345947265625, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.530589710582387, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='cytosol and 0.021 for organelles, Extended Data Fig. 2). In addition to the two segmentation tasks \ndescribed here, the 2D CNN has the potential to segment individual organelle types when provided with \nsufficient amounts of training data (Extended Data Table 1). Operating in 2D rather than 3D results in \nonly little memory requirement (<1GB at the default batch size) and short training time (~15 minutes to \ntrain on ten tomograms using a Nvidia V100 GPU; Online Methods). \n \nHyperparameter tuning for the 3D CNN \nThe 3D CNN is built on a flexible architecture for which we performed hyperparameter tuning. We \ncharacterized the effect of varying the adjustable hyperparameters (Online Methods) on the DeePiCt \nworkflow using a CV approach for RIBO, FAS and membranes in the VPP dataset, and for NPC in the \nDEF* dataset.  \n \nIn each case we combined the 3D CNN segmentation of the target structure with an appropriate region \nmask (Supplementary Fig. 1, Supplementary Table 1) and determined the 3D CNN hyperparameter \ncombination that optimizes the task of localization or segmentation (Extended Data Fig. 6, \nSupplementary Table 1). Our results show that the best hyperparameter selection for the 3D CNN is \nstructure-dependent, and not only related to the size of the complex or to the receptive field of the network \n(a function of the depth parameter D), but also to the particle’s density, symmetry and abundance, among \nothers. Therefore, the architecture flexibility of our implementation of the 3D CNN is essential for the \naccurate segmentation of a diverse set of biological structures. In particular, BN shortens the learning speed \n(and notably improves the performance results in the case of NPCs and FAS). Dropout layers and a number \nof data augmentation strategies (Gaussian noise, salt-and-pepper noise, rotations, elastic transformations, \nsee Supplementary Note 1) did not improve performance. \n \nPerformance of DeePiCt in the same-domain setting \nOnce the 3D CNN architectures which optimized the tasks of particle localization (for FAS and RIBO) and \nstructure segmentation (membranes and NPC) were determined, we analyzed the performance of the \nDeePiCt workflow in the same-domain setting (i.e. training and testing in the same domain). The 3-fold \nCV tests in VPP (Fig. 3b, Extended Data Fig. 7a-c), showed that it achieved a performance F1 score \nbetween 0.68-0.80 for RIBO (median F1 of 0.79), 0.21-0.64 for FAS (median F1 of 0.46) and 0.58-0.90 for \nmembranes (median voxel-F1 of 0.71). The results in DEF* for NPC segmentation (Fig. 3c) show that, \neven if the network is trained on the whole DEF* dataset (23% of which is high quality data, Online \nMethods), the performance of the method is clearly different in the high- versus lower-quality DEF* \ntomograms: with a median voxel-F1 of 0.47 in high-quality data, while a median voxel-F1 of 0.19 in lower-\nquality DEF* tomograms. \n \nComparison of DeePiCt to state-of-the-art particle localization tools \nTo benchmark DeePiCt in the particle localization task, we compared it to TM and DeepFinder in the tasks \nof RIBO and FAS in the VPP dataset (Fig. 3d,e). Following the suggestions by the authors28, DeepFinder \nwas trained in a multi-class fashion, where both particles were learnt simultaneously by a single network. \nFor the training, we used the same 3-fold CV splitting of the VPP datasets as for DeePiCt. In RIBO \nlocalization, DeepFinder achieved a median F1 of 0.83, comparable to DeePiCt. For FAS localization, \nDeepFinder achieved a median F1 of 0.11 (significantly below DeePiCt, t-test p-value of 0.007; Fig. 3d). \nIt is worth mentioning that single-class DeepFinder networks tested for FAS localization completely failed \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7683715820312, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.76837158203125, 'height': 639.10595703125, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.525135387073863, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='by not localizing any particles. DeePiCt needs ~17h for training vs ~3h for DeepFinder, while predicting \nand post processing is equally fast for both (~500 clusters/minute) (Online Methods). Furthermore, \nDeePiCt’s trained networks (models) for all structures mentioned in this work are open source and publicly \navailable (Code and data availability).  \n \nFinally, the analysis of TM performance was done with the raw output with respect to the top 2000 peaks \nin the case of RIBO and to the top 1000 peaks in the case of FAS. TM also takes several hours (s. parameters \nin Online Methods) and our results confirm that TM has lower performance than both deep-learning \nmethods for the localization of RIBO and FAS, completely failing at the latter (Fig. 3e).  \n \nDomain generalization of DeePiCt across image acquisition conditions \nThe DEF data is characterized by a lower SNR and lower contrast in the image, as compared to VPP \n(Supplementary Note 2). This makes the task of particle localization and structure segmentation more \nchallenging, but has the advantage that the resulting subtomogram averages generally provide better \nresolutions43. The comprehensive ground truth construction in DEF enabled us to study the generalization \npotential of the DeePiCt workflow across image acquisition conditions, from VPP to DEF data. To that \nend, we predicted cytosol, organelles, membranes, FAS and RIBO in 10 DEF tomograms, with networks \ntrained in the yeast VPP data (Fig. 3f-g).  \n \nCompared to segmentation of VPP data, the 2D CNN shows slightly poorer performance on DEF data for \ncytosol (Fig. 3a,f). By employing amplitude spectrum equalization for the 2D CNN, the domain \ngeneralization capabilities greatly improved for both cytosol (from initial median AUPRC=0.82 to median \nAUPRC=0.95) and organelles (from AUPRC=0.42 to 0.74) (Fig. 3f, Extended Data Fig. 5). For DeePiCt, \nthe performance scores in this domain generalization setting also showed a decay with respect to the same \ndomain setting: with a median F1 of 0.59 for RIBO, a median F1 of 0.15 for FAS, and a median voxel-F1 \nof 0.41 for membranes (Fig. 3g). Importantly, as in the case of the 2D CNN, the spectrum equalization filter \nin the preprocessing step improved the domain generalization results (Extended Data Fig. 7d-f). \nFurthermore, we confirmed that using the cytosol predictions as a region mask to clean particle annotations \nalso improved the performances (Extended Data Fig. 7g-h). An example of DeePict segmentation outcome \nin the cross-domain setting is shown in Fig. 4a-b and visually resembles the ground truth annotation \n(Extended Data Fig. 8a). The DeePiCt predictions and the ground truth annotations enabled us to analyze \nthe DEF tomograms in the same way as we had done for the VPP data, by downstream 3D structural \nclassification and refinement (Fig. 5).  \n \nDeePiCt predictions result in high quality subtomogram averages  \nIn the case of FAS in the DEF dataset, fewer particles were predicted than in the ground truth (Extended \nData Tables 5, 6). Nevertheless, the subtomogram average of the detected particles revealed the two half \ndomes of the barrel-shaped type I FAS with applied D3 symmetry, consistent with the ground truth average \nand published structures of this fungal 2.6-megadalton heterododecameric (α6β6) complex from other yeast \nspecies (Fig. 4c-d, Extended Data Fig. 8b-e). The phosphopantetheine transferase (PPT) domains required \nfor activation of FAS could be observed along the equatorial plane of the complex. In addition, three \nequatorial densities were resolved that could not be assigned based on published structures. Three densities \ninside each half dome connected to the central α wheel and in close proximity to the ketosynthase (KS) fit \nwith the acyl carrier protein (ACP) of Saccharomyces cerevisiae44 (Fig. 4d, PDB: 2uv8) and Pichia \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7681274414062, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.76812744140625, 'height': 640.3059692382812, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.55240839177912, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='pastoris45 (Extended Data Fig. 8c, EMD-12139). The ACP shuttles the growing acyl-chain between the \ndifferent catalytic sites, and its localization has been suggested in connection with the activity of the whole \nmulti-enzyme complex44,46–49. Here, S. pombe FAS complexes within FIB-lamellae prepared from \nexponentially growing cells revealed a specific ACP site, which had not been observed by cryo-ET and \nthus in its native state before (Fig. 4d, Extended Data Fig. 8, Supplementary Note 2). \n \nFor ribosomes, the particle averages and numbers purely derived from DeePiCt predictions are comparable \nto the ground truth annotations in DEF (Extended Data Tables 5, 6; Supplementary Note 2, \nSupplementary Fig. 4, 5) complementing the performance analysis results described above. 3D \nrefinements of these lamella-derived ribosomal particles from both ground truth and DeePiCt resulted in \nsubtomogram averages with the nominal resolutions of 11 Å and 15 Å after multi-particle refinement in \nM33, respectively (Fig. 4e, Extended Data Fig. 8f, Extended Data Tables 5, 6). Hierarchical 3D \nclassification revealed in both datasets a well-aligned class that was further refined in M to subnanometer \nresolutions of 9.3 Å and 9.4 Å for ground truth and DeePiCt predictions, respectively (Fig. 4f-h, Extended \nData Fig. 8i-k, Extended Data Tables 5, 6, Supplementary Note 2, Supplementary Fig. 4, 5). This \nallowed the identification of tRNA occupying the P-site of the peptidyl transferase center (PTC) as well as \nthe L1 stalk facing the E-site (Fig. 4g-h, Extended Data Fig. 8j-k).  \n \nDeePiCt-predicted ribosomes reveal functional sub-populations \nThe large number of particles that DeePiCt localized in DEF in a high throughput manner allows for \nexamining subpopulations of macromolecular complexes that are functionally distinct by downstream \nsubtomogram analysis. Further classification of all DeePiCt-predicted ribosomes focused on the head of \nthe 40S small subunit revealed a subset with additional densities close to the head and at the exit tunnel \n(Fig. 4i, Supplementary Fig. 6). This class was also detected in VPP and in DEF ground truth datasets \n(Extended Data Tables 4-6). In the DEF ground truth dataset, densities for P- and E-site tRNAs and the \nL1 stalk facing inwards are resolved (Extended Data Fig. 9a-e). The ribosome-bound ATPase eEF3 from \nS. cerevisiae50 fitted well into the additional head density (CC 0.8972, EMD-12062, Fig. 5i, Extended Data \nFig. 9 a-c). During translation, this eukaryotic elongation factor facilitates binding of a new tRNA to the \nA-site of the ribosome via the ternary aminoacyl-tRNA–eEF1A–GTP complex50,51. In addition, unassigned \ndensity at the ribosomal exit tunnel is observable for this class. Focused classification of all DeePiCt-\npredicted ribosomes at this location revealed a subtomogram average fitting the S. cerevisiae ribosome52 \n(CC 0.9657, EMD-1667) with the rRNA expansion segment ES27L in a particular configuration53 (CC \n0.7938, PDB-3izd) connecting to an additional density close to the ribosomal exit tunnel (Fig. 4j, \nSupplementary Fig. 7). ES27L plays a role in translation fidelity and recruits enzymes to the ribosomal \nexit tunnel, such as the methionine aminopeptidase (MetAP) that co-translationally processes the nascent \npeptide chain54,55. The nuclear export factor Arx1, which is released during cytosolic ribosomal 60S \nmaturation in S. cerevisiae56, and its human homologue Ebp1, a translation regulator57,58, similarly bind at \nlocations of the observed extra density (Fig. 4j, Extended Data Fig. 9h). These binding factors recruit the \nflexible rRNA scaffold ES27L and cover the ribosomal exit tunnel with their MetAP-like folds. This \nstructural class was also detected in DEF gt and VPP gt datasets (Extended Data Fig. 9f-j, Extended Data \nTables 4-5).  \n \nThus, DeePiCt predictions combined with structural analysis revealed functional subpopulations of \nribosomes. Mapping them back into the tomograms revealed their 3D spatial distributions in relation to \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.766845703125, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.766845703125, 'height': 638.5902099609375, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.51341386274858, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content="other macromolecules such as FAS and organelles in the cellular context (Fig. 4b). \n \nDeePiCt's combination of organelle segmentation and high-throughput particle localization \nenables structural insights into ribosome-mitochondria association  \nOne advantage of DeePiCt over state-of-the-art tools is that it allows studying particle populations within \ntheir cellular context, by recovering particles in close proximity to specific organelles selected from \npredictions by the 2D CNN. We recovered cytosolic ribosomes within 25 nm distance to predicted \nendoplasmic reticulum (ER) and mitochondria from 7 and 3 DEF tomograms, respectively. Using \nsubtomogram analyses for both subsets, two classes could be separated, one with and one without a \nmembrane density (Supplementary Fig. 8-9). In the first class, ribosomes associated with the membrane \nin a specific orientation, whereas they were randomly oriented in the second class. The latter class likely \narises from the highly crowded nature of the S. pombe cytoplasm (Fig. 4k-l), which in the case of DeePiCt \npredictions in DEF data, is also affected by imperfect organelle segmentations (Fig. 4b). ER-bound \nribosomes faced the membrane with the ribosomal exit tunnel in agreement with published structures from \nother species such as S. cerevisiae1,52,59 (Extended Data Fig. 9 k-n, Extended Data Table 6). \nMitochondria-bound ribosomes, which posed a particular challenge to structural analysis in previous \nstudies59,60, were found to interact with the membrane at an angular offset of around 35° in comparison to \nthe ER-bound ribosomes (Fig. 4m-n, Extended Data Fig. 9o-r, Extended Data Table 6). Interestingly, a \ndensity connecting the ribosome to the mitochondrial membrane was detected at the bottom of the large \nsubunit in close proximity to the small subunit, but not to the ribosomal exit tunnel. It possibly represents \nthe ribosome nascent chain complex (NAC) in contact with the mitochondrion receptor OM1461–63 which \nhas yet to be structurally described.  \n \nIn summary, ribosomes close to mitochondria and ER exhibit different interfaces with the respective \nmembranes, facilitating specific protein nascent chain membrane insertion or transfer into the \norganelle2,52,62. These results highlight the power of DeePiCt for high-throughput particle localization and \ncryo-electron tomogram segmentation to rapidly gain new biological insights. \n \nTrained networks can be readily applied to other species \nAs a demonstration of the domain generalization potential of our workflow across species, we predicted \nsegmentations of ribosomes, membranes and cytoskeleton structures (actin filaments and microtubules) in \na published VPP HeLa cell dataset1 (Fig. 5). The application of the DeePiCt pipeline employed a manually \ngenerated mask using Amira64 of the cytoplasmic volume, excluding the nucleus. For the evaluation of our \nresults, we computed the voxel-F1 score in comparison to the publicly available annotations (EMD-\n119921). For ribosomes and membranes, we used the ribosome- and membrane-networks trained on the 10 \nVPP-tomograms from S. pombe described in previous sections. This resulted in a voxel-F1 of 0.55 for \nribosomes and a voxel-F1 of 0.18 for membranes (Extended Data Fig. 10a). However, the available expert \nmembrane segmentation for the dataset covered only ER membranes, which makes the voxel-F1 value less \nmeaningful. Visual inspection of the predicted membrane segmentation revealed a good fit (Fig. 5). \n \nAdditionally we generated models for microtubules prediction, initially training a network for simultaneous \nactin and microtubule segmentations in 4 tomograms of cryo-FIB milled Human retinal pigment epithelial-\n1 (RPE-1) cells65. Due to the high preferential orientation of the cytoskeletal filaments in this data exhibiting \nstress fibers, the performance for microtubules’ segmentation was low resulting in a voxel-F1 score of 0.26 \n", metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.9998779296875, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.9998779296875, 'height': 640.3059692382812, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.55240839177912, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='(Extended Data Fig. 10a). We therefore trained a second 3D CNN for microtubule segmentation on 11 \ntomograms from lamella of cryo-FIB-milled, dissociated C. elegans cells, containing 890 microtubules in \na wide range of orientations. This new microtubule segmentation network achieved a voxel-F1 score of \n0.83 (Extended Data Fig. 10a), exemplifying the importance of a training set with a high number of \nstructures, as well as orientations to mitigate the effect of the missing wedge on the prediction performance.  \n \nFinally, we trained a dedicated network for predicting actin-filaments on 5 manually curated tomograms, 2 \nfrom RPE-1 cells and 3 from Mouse embryonic fibroblasts (MEF) 3T3 cells, that contained approximately \n3,740 actin filaments (Online Methods). The actin filaments network shows a low voxel-F1 score of 0.10 \nin the HeLa cell tomogram (Fig. 5, Extended Data Fig. 10a), which is likely caused by the finer structure \nof actin in comparison to microtubules, the minimal set of orientations sampled in the small amount of \ntraining data, and to the fact that most training filaments are arranged in bundles, probably causing the CNN \nto learn this superstructure rather than individual filaments (Extended Data Fig. 10b). \n \nOverall, these results show that the prediction of ribosomes and microtubules, representing large \nmacromolecular complexes, is especially well-preserved across species and that the availability of diverse \nand well-annotated training datasets are crucial for good performance of DeePiCt. The results shown in \nFig. 5 highlight the use of trained networks from DeePiCt to segment novel datasets spanning different \nspecies.  \n \nDiscussion \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 545.6400146484375, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 473.6400146484375, 'height': 307.5884704589844, 'nlines': 21, 'fs': 14.647070021856399, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='Our DeePiCt workflow facilitates accurate and fast localization of diverse structures in cryo-ET of intact \ncells. The demonstrated high performance and the flexibility of the 3D CNN architecture offer the user a \nreliable tool for pattern recognition. This enabled us to detect particle species, such as FAS, that are low in \nabundance and have a less dense structural signature in comparison to ribosomes. The integration of \nstructure segmentation (predicted by the 3D CNN) with the contextual information excludes false positives \nin the 3D localization of particles and structures segmentation task, and harnesses the cellular context to \ncarry out spatial studies focused on regions of biological interest. This enabled us to investigate the 3D \nspatial distribution of macromolecules such as ribosomes in proximity to specific organelles (e.g. \nmitochondria) and to obtain structural insights with functional implications. \n \nHere, we provide the first publicly available experimental cryo-ET dataset of 20 S. pombe tomograms under \ntwo microscopy acquisition settings (VPP and DEF), together with a high-quality comprehensive \nannotation of RIBO and FAS, membranes, organelle and cytosol segmentations. This constitutes the first \ngold-standard dataset in the field that is large enough for unbiased model training, which will enable \nbenchmarking of current methods, and spur the development of future computational tools for unbiased \ndata mining in cryo-ET data.  \n \nSubtomogram averaging of the annotated particles from either ground truth or DeePiCt predictions resulted \nin the first density maps of the S. pombe ribosome and fatty acid synthase. We observed differences in the \nanalysis of subtomograms derived from either VPP and DEF tomograms, despite both being derived from \nwild-type S. pombe cryo-FIB lamellae (Supplementary Note 2). Due to the preservation of high frequency \nfeatures in DEF data, a subset of well-aligned ribosomes could be identified and refined to subnanometer \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7723388671875, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.7723388671875, 'height': 317.5059509277344, 'nlines': 22, 'fs': 14.43208867853338, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content="resolution densities from cryo-FIB lamellae. The obtained maps provide detailed structural insights; for \nexample, the P-site tRNA occupying the PTC, which together with the elongation factor eEF3 detected \nclose to the head of the 40S subunit in a subset of ribosomes, suggest active translation. Further \nclassifications close to the ribosomal exit tunnel revealed ES27L in a specific configuration providing a \nplatform for other factors to interact with the exit tunnel and a potentially protruding nascent chain. By \nutilizing the contextual information of the DeePiCt predictions, differences in relative membrane \norientation of mitochondria- and ER-bound S. pombe ribosomes were detected. The observed connecting \ndensity between ribosomes and mitochondria likely highlights the not yet structurally characterized \nribosome-bound NAC in contact with OM-1461–63  facilitating protein import into mitochondria60,61.  \n \nThe analysis of DeePiCt's performance confirmed that data quality is an important factor in its predictive \nability, as demonstrated in the case of NPC predictions in the DEF* dataset, which we classified into high \nand lower quality subsets based on lamella thickness and tilt-series alignment error. As the NPC has a high \ndegree of structural flexibility on the subunit and diameter level inside cells40 it is a more challenging target \nthan for example ribosomes and thus emphasizes the importance of higher quality data to achieve sufficient \nperformance. The introduction of a pre-processing equalizing filter is also an important element of the \npipeline that improves both the learning process during the training of 3D segmentation networks for \nparticles with less dense print than the ribosome (e.g. FAS), and especially the generalization power across \ndomains, including different microscopy acquisition conditions. Thus, amplitude spectrum equalization \nallows localizing macromolecules across different data acquisition types. In the case of the 2D network, \nalthough pre-processing did not improve model performance on the same-domain inference, it led to cross-\ndomain improvements when training on VPP data and inferring on DEF data, or when training on both data \ntypes combined to segment organelles and cytosol. More elaborate tasks, such as prediction of individual \norganelle types, will likely require more training data or training of a dedicated 3D CNN with a tailored \nnetwork architecture. \n \nOur workflow allows easy adaptation to the segmentations of other structures, as demonstrated by the \napplication of cytoskeleton segmentation networks. The networks show high-quality performance for \nmicrotubules in the HeLa cell dataset after training on data with broad orientation sampling of the filaments. \nActin predictions revealed a low F1 score and therefore likely require more training data sampling different \norientations of the structural features to improve performance. Altogether, the application of multiple \nsegmentation networks to the HeLa cell dataset revealed that DeePiCt trained on datasets from different \nmicroscopes, species, and conditions lead to reasonably good results when the quality of data is high. \nAlthough more in-depth analysis is needed to study the specific limitations for the applicability of DeePiCt \nnetworks on other datasets, the results presented here constitute the first step towards conducting large-\nscale quantitative analyses for structural biology using cryo-ET on cells from different laboratories and \nopen source datasets. In this sense, the generated ground truth annotations in this study are a starting point \nto provide the community with a resource to improve and further develop cryo-ET object segmentation and \ndetection tools, which will ultimately enable further exploration of particles in their cellular context. \nTogether with the trained networks and their demonstrated applicability to other species, as well as the \nflexibility of the DeePiCt workflow, the software harbors great potential for quantitative cryo-ET studies \nin the future. \n \n", metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.77099609375, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.77099609375, 'height': 623.2659301757812, 'nlines': 43, 'fs': 14.494556515715843, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),

The CNN U-Net architecture: the 2D \nnetwork performs all tensor operations on the two-dimensional spatial coordinates, with D=5 and IF=16; the 3D \nnetwork performs tensor operations on the three spatial dimensions, and the architectural hyperparameters in red can \nbe set by the user. b. The DeePiCt pipeline is used to train and predict various structural features in cryo-electron \ntomograms. b.1 The DeePiCt pipeline consists of two independent CNNs: a 2D network for compartment \nsegmentation and a 3D network for particle localization. b.2 Trained networks are applied to input tomograms which \ncan be pre-processed with a spectrum-matching filter to improve image contrast.  Document(page_content='Yeast cell culture \nS. pombe K972 Sp h- wt haploid was kindly provided by C. Haering, originally from P. Nurse. Cells were \nrecovered from frozen stock by streaking on YE5S agar plates (YES Broth, Formedium, 20 g agarose/L) \nand incubated at 30 °C for 1-3 days. Colonies were re-streaked on fresh YES agar plates and incubated 1-\n3 days at 30 °C. Single colonies were inoculated in 5 mL of YES medium (YES Broth, Formedium, \nPCM0302, FM0618/8573) and grown at 30 °C, 170 rpm overnight (NCU-Shaker mini, Benchmark). On \nthe next day, cultures were grown to their log phase at OD600 of 0.5 - 0.6 and diluted beforehand in YES if \nnecessary.  \n  \nVitrification of yeast cells \nA Leica EM GP (Leica Microsystems) was utilized to vitrify yeast cells at liquid nitrogen temperature. \nYeast cells were either diluted to OD600 of 0.2-0.4 in YES medium or, following a prior wash step, in PBS \ncontaining 5 % or 10 % BSA as cryoprotectant. In the chamber, 4 µl were directly applied to TEM grids \n(Quantifoil R1/2, Cu 200 mesh, holey carbon or SiO2 film), which were glow discharged on both sides for \n45 s (Pelco Easy glow) immediately before usage. Blotting from the back side of the support was performed \nfor 1-2 s at 22 °C and 99% humidity. Cells were plunge-frozen in liquid ethane cooled by liquid nitrogen \nand transferred into grid boxes until further usage. \n  \nCryo-FIB of S. pombe \nTEM grids containing vitrified yeast cells were clipped into an autogrid with a cut-out enabling cryo-\nfocused ion beam (cryo-FIB) milling at shallow angles67. Mounted on a 45° pre-tilt shuttle, grids were \ntransferred into an Aquilos Dual beam microscope (ThermoFisher Scientific). Prior to cryo-FIB milling at \nliquid nitrogen temperature, cells were sputter-coated with platinum for 10-15 s (1 kV, 10 mA, 10 Pa). \nSubsequently, a layer of organometallic platinum (reservoir at 28°C) was applied by opening the gas \ninjection system (GIS) for 8 sec at a stage Z position of 3-4 mm below the coincidence point. In three \nindependent sessions, three grids with 5 lamellae each were prepared at a milling angle of 15°. \nAgglomerations of several cells were micromachined in three steps of rough milling to a thickness of 5 µm \nat 1 nA ion beam current, 3 µm at 0.5 nA and 1 µm at 0.1 nA. Milling progress was visually monitored \nbetween each milling step with the scanning electron microscope beam (SEM, 10 kV, 50 pA). Fine milling \nwas performed at 50 pA to a target thickness of 200 nm. To render the lamellae conductive for TEM \nimaging, they were sputtered with platinum for 5 s (1 kV, 10 mA, 10 Pa) and transferred into cryo boxes. \n  \nCryo-electron tomography of S. pombe \nAutogrids containing lamellae were loaded into a Titan Krios (Thermo Fisher Scientific) such that the axis \nof the pre-tilt introduced by FIB milling was aligned perpendicular to the tilt axis of the microscope68. Cryo-\nelectron tomography acquisition parameters are summarized in Extended Data Tables 4-5. Tomograms \nwere acquired at 300 kV on a K2 Summit direct detection camera (Gatan) operating in dose fractionation \nmode and utilizing a Quantum post-column energy filter operated at zero-loss (Gatan). Magnification of \n42,000 (EFTEM) with a calibrated pixel size of 3.37 Å was used for the ground truth VPP and DEF datasets \nand 3.45 Å for NPC data. Up to 14 tilt series were collected on a single lamella in low dose mode using \nSerialEM69. Starting from the lamella pre-tilt, images were acquired at 2.0 - 4.0 µm underfocus, in 2° \nincrements within a range of + 58° to - 40° using a dose-symmetric tilt scheme70. The total dose was up to \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.7710571289062, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.77105712890625, 'height': 612.7059936523438, 'nlines': 42, 'fs': 14.588237944103422, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),

 Document(page_content='RIBO and FAS were localized in 4-times binned tomograms (13.48 Å pixel size) in an iterative workflow. \nTM (RIBO) or manual annotations (FAS) (step 1, Extended Data Tables 2-3) were used for training 3D \nCNNs (step 2). CNN predictions were masked with a segmentation of the cytosol. Step 2 was repeated \nseveral times and each training round trained on the combined predictions of step 1 and the preceding round. \nEach round in step 2 consisted of three simultaneously trained networks sharing the same set of default \nhyperparameters (Supplementary Tables 2-3), except for the number of IF=4, 8, and 32, to provide a \ncumulative prediction less overfitted to the incomplete training data. The cumulative total predictions from \nsteps 1 and 2 were then complemented by a round of visual inspection to eliminate false positives and \nmanual picking to recover remaining false negatives (step 3, Extended Data Tables 2-3).  Document(page_content='D3 symmetry using the S. cerevisiae FAS map (EMD-162346, low-pass filtered to 60 Å) as reference. \nHierarchical 3D classifications (25 iterations each) were performed either after 3D refinements in RELION \n(VPP data) or after subtomogram re-extraction using the ribosome-optimized image and volume models in \nM (described above). \n \nFinal subtomogram averages of each particle class were obtained by 3D refinement and post-processing, \nincluding filtering to their respective resolutions determined by Fourier Shell Correlation (FSC) of two \nindependently refined half maps at a cut-off of 0.143. Details of particle numbers and resolutions for each \nsubtomogram average are summarized in Extended Data Tables 4-6. Visualization and calculation of \ncross-correlations (CC) between different maps and models, was performed with the UCSF ChimeraX \npackage82.  \n \nCNN Pre- and post-processing \nPreprocessing \nTomograms are normalized to obtain uniform mean of 0 and variance of 1 in the frequency domain, prior \nto training. Additionally, the spectrum equalization filter is applied by matching the amplitude spectrum of \nthe tomogram that is to be transformed to the target spectrum of one manually selected high-contrast VPP \ntomogram (Tomogram TS_001, Extended Data Fig. 1b). Extraction of spectra amplitudes is done using \nfast Fourier transform (FFT) followed by radially averaging amplitudes across the frequency domain. If the \ntarget tomogram’s Nyquist frequency is lower than the input tomogram’s, the target spectrum is padded \nwith zeros to match the input spectrum’s size. Next, an equalization vector is created by dividing entry-\nwise the target spectrum by the respective input spectrum. The resulting equalization vector is converted \ninto a rotational kernel and multiplied by the input tomograms in the frequency domain in combination with \na sigmoidal-shaped low-pass filter to eliminate high frequency noise. After back transformation, the \ntomogram exhibits a similar contrast to the target tomogram (Extended Data Fig. 1). Tomogram intensities \nare additionally normalized to obtain uniform mean and variance. For the 2D CNN, tomograms and training \nsegmentations are processed slice-wise into 2D tiles with a fixed size of 288x288 pixels (256x256 pixels \nand 16 pixels padding on each side). For the 3D CNN, tomograms are by default split into cubic patches of \n64x64x64 voxels, and 12 voxels overlap in each dimension.  \n \nPost-processing (2D CNN) \nThe post-processing steps for the 2D network are focused on assembling the per-slice prediction into a 3D \nsegmentation. To that aim, predicted tile segmentations are cropped on each side by 48 pixels to reduce \nartifacts around the edges, followed by reassembly into 3D stacks, with remaining overlapping areas being \naveraged. A 1D Gaussian filter is successively applied along the z axis in order to reduce single-slice false \npositives (Extended Data Fig. 2). \n \nPost-processing (3D CNN) \nAfter reassembly of the individual 64x64x64 voxel patches into the probability map resulting from the 3D \nCNN, the post processing step includes the clustering of the thresholded map (usually at threshold value of \n0.5). According to user preferences, only clusters that lie within or close to a given mask consisting of an \norganelle or cytosol segmentation outputted by the 2D network, and whose size lies within a given range \nof numbers of voxels, are considered for the final prediction map (Supplementary Note 1). This ‘cleaning’ \nprocedure allows adding a priori information on the size of the particles in question. Additionally, for \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 542.76904296875, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 470.76904296875, 'height': 638.5902099609375, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.51341386274858, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),

 Document(page_content='macromolecular complex localization, the user has the option to export a list of coordinates of cluster-\ncentroids, which represent the particles’ locations prediction. \n \nPrecision-Recall and Performance Analysis \nCross-validation approach \nThe analysis of performance for RIBO and FAS localization, as well as membrane segmentation, were done \nusing a 3-fold CV scheme, where 8 out of the 10 VPP tomograms were used for training, and the remaining \n2 VPP tomograms were used for testing. In the test for domain generalization, the same three networks \n(trained on the 8 VPP tomograms) were applied to the 10 DEF tomograms. \n \nThe analysis of performance in the NPC segmentation task was done in the dedicated DEF* of 127 \ntomograms. Precision and recall \n(Supplementary Note 1) were computed at threshold values varying from 0 to 1 on the picked voxels to \ncompute the area under the precision-recall curves (AURPC) for each tomogram.  \n \nEvaluation metrics \nIn the case of particles (RIBO and FAS), the localization evaluation was made directly from the predicted \nlists containing their centroids’ voxel coordinates. In the case of DeePict, the lists of particles’ coordinates \nare sorted according to a decreasing score (cluster size) value. For both RIBO and FAS, we defined a true \npositive when a predicted coordinate matched a ground-truth particle coordinate within a tolerance radius \nof 130 Å (Supplementary Note 1). The F1 score, which is calculated as the harmonic mean of the precision \nand the recall, provides a measure of the quality of the predictions. We chose this measure for easier \ncomparison to previous studies for particle localization methods in the field of cryo-ET26,27,29,83. Other \nmetrics such as the area under the precision recall curve (AUPRC) generated by increasing length of \nparticles’ list were disregarded as the ordering of the list (a function of cluster size) does not necessarily \nreflect the method’s confidence on positive classification. \n \nThe evaluation of the segmentation task is of a totally different nature, as the shape of the object should be \ntaken into account voxel-wise. For the 2D CNN predictions of organelles and cytosol, we used the AUPRC \nscore with varying threshold over the network’s output map (Supplementary Note 1). For Deepict’s \npredictions of NPC and membrane segmentation, we instead used the voxel-based F1 score \n(Supplementary Note 1), to maintain hegemony with DeePiCt particle prediction evaluations. \nHyperparameter tuning of DeePiCt and performance assessment \nThe previously described cross-validation schemes and evaluation metrics were not only used for the final \nassessment of methods’ performance (Fig. 3), but to study the effect of the different hyperparameters that \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 545.4000244140625, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 473.4000244140625, 'height': 637.6659545898438, 'nlines': 44, 'fs': 14.492408058860086, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),

 Document(page_content='The general architecture of the 3D and 2D CNN is based also on the U-Net architecture1 and consists \nof two consecutive symmetric paths: an encoder and a decoder path connected by skip-connections and \na transitional base-layer (Fig. 1a), where:  \n \n(a) The encoder path is a sequence of alternated convolutional blocks and max-pooling layers. In turn, \nthe convolutional blocks are a series of convolutions (kernel size 𝑘" = "3, stride 𝑠 = 1 and zero padding \n𝑝" = "2) followed by a rectified linear unit (ReLU). In the case of the 3D CNN, optional batch \nnormalization (BN) and dropout layers can be alternated (Fig. 1a). The max-pooling layer has fixed \nwindow size 𝑤 = 2 and stride 𝑠 = 2. \nIn our implementations, the initial convolutional block has a variable number of initial filters (IF) in its \nfirst convolutional layer, which must be set by the user. In all cases, subsequent convolutional blocks \nduplicate the number of feature maps from the previous level. The number of downsampling layers plus \n1, is referred to as the network’s depth (D), which is also a hyperparameter to be set by the user. It \ndetermines the size of the receptive field (𝑅𝐹) of the network (𝑅𝐹" = "2 ∗ 𝐷). For the 2D CNN, the \ndepth is a fixed value 𝐷 = 5. \n \n(b) The base of the CNNs is the transitional convolutional layer between the encoder and decoder paths.  \n \n(c) The decoder path is symmetric with respect to the encoder path, and is a sequence of alternated \nconvolutional and upsampling (via transposed convolution) layers2, with kernel size 𝑘 = 3, stride 𝑠 =\n1 and padding 𝑝 = 1. For the 3D CNN, analogous optional layers can be alternated between \nconvolutional and upsampling blocks (BN and dropout). The encoder and decoder paths’ associated \nparameters are denoted throughout the text as ED and DD, respectively. \nEssential to the U-Net architecture are the skip-connections, which consist of concatenating the feature \nmaps from the encoder to the decoder path at every level. The last activation layer is in our case a \nsigmoid function, compatible with our choice of the Dice Loss function3. By definition, this is a multi-\nlabel approach, where single voxels may belong to multiple semantic classes, and which frees the user \nfrom defining additional weighting parameters to compensate for class imbalance necessary for other \nloss function choices such as categorical cross-entropy. In our experience, the use of Dice Loss provided \nbetter qualitative results in all cases. \nAll models were trained using Adam Optimiser4. Application of these networks to unseen tomograms \nrequires scaling of their pixel size to match (at least approximately) that of the training data. \n \nData Augmentation \nFor the 2D CNN, tiles are randomly flipped and rotated in 90-degree increments during training to \nimprove generalization.  \n \nAlthough it did not provide improved results in our hands (not shown), data augmentation for training \nthe 3D CNN is available and optional. The number of times that each of the original training examples \nis augmented is given by a parameter 𝑛01 that can be set by the user. Each augmentation includes 4 \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 529.0800170898438, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 457.08001708984375, 'height': 585.4017333984375, 'nlines': 40, 'fs': 14.635043334960937, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),
 Document(page_content='different types of random volumetric transformations (applied to the original image and the labels, \nexcept in the case of noise), that we describe in what follows. Let A in 𝑅2×2×2 be a single channel \ncubic image of side-length 𝑛 > 0. Then:  \n \n1. Additive Gaussian noise. Given a predefined modulation factor 𝜖" > "0 , the Additive Gaussian \nnoise transformation 𝐺8 is defined by 𝐺8(𝐴)"= "𝐴" + "𝜖𝐵, where 𝐵>?@ ~ 𝑁(0, 1) are independent and \nidentically distributed random variables (i.i.d.r.v.), and 𝑁(𝜇, 𝜎E) denotes a normally distributed \nGaussian random variable with mean 𝜇 and variance 𝜎E. \n \n2. Salt-and-pepper noise. Given user-defined modulation factor 𝑎 > 0 and a probability rate 1 > 𝑝" >\n"0 , the salt-and-pepper noise transformation is defined by 𝑆H,I(𝐴)"= "𝐴" + "𝑎 ∗ 𝐵, where 𝐵>?@ ~ \n𝑈(𝑝, [−1, 1]) are i.i.d.r.v., and 𝑈(𝑝, 𝐼) denotes a uniformly distributed random variable in a real interval \n𝐼. \n \n3. Elastic transformation. Given coordinate entries of A that are located at a coarse regular grid of \nthickness 𝑛O > 1 are displaced by a random vector of i.i.d.r.v. uniform random variables. To achieve a \nsmooth displacement vector field for all the points of the image 𝐴 (i.e. on the fine grid), we generate the \nelastically transformed image as the interpolated vector field (via polynomial interpolation of order 𝑘 >\n0, defined by the user). \n \n4. Random rotation. Given a probability of rotation 𝑝 ∈ (0, 1) and angle range 𝛼 ∈ (0, 180), the image \nA is rotated with a probability 𝑝 with and angle in [−𝛼, 𝛼], with respect to the 𝑧 −axis.  \n \n3D CNN default hyperparameters \nA default, starting point set of hyperparameters values for the 3D CNN for this study: 𝐷 = 2, 𝐼𝐹 = 4, \n𝐵𝑁 = 0, 𝐸𝐷 = 0, 𝐷𝐷 = 0, and 𝑛01 = 0.  Document(page_content='Cryo-ET datasets of S. pombe utilized in this study were derived from cryo-FIB lamellae of wild-type, \nnative, yeast cells. 20 tomograms used for comprehensive annotations were acquired with a dose-\nsymmetric tilt scheme6 employing similar acquisition parameters (Extended Data Tables 4-6, Online \nMethods), with the only difference being imaging with traditional defocus-only (DEF) or, in addition, \nthe usage of a VPP. All RIBO and FAS particles annotated in the two datasets (DEF and VPP) were \nsubjected to subtomogram analysis including CTF correction utilizing CTF fitting and model creation \nin Warp, and 3D refinements, hierarchical, and focused 3D classifications in RELION. During this \nstructural analysis we made several observations which will be discussed in the following.  \n  \nAs described previously7,8,9, acquiring tomography data with a VPP emphasizes low frequencies. \nPronounced low frequencies lead to improved image contrast which can facilitate data mining. With \nour method, more particles were detected in VPP than in DEF ground truth annotations of FAS \ncomplexes, which represent more challenging targets for data mining due to their low abundance and \nhollow structural signature. The increased SNR in VPP was also advantageous in hierarchical \nclassifications of ribosomes (Supplementary Fig. 2), while no difference was apparent for FAS likely \ndue to the low particle numbers (Supplementary Fig. 3). For VPP, all ribosomes clustered in defined \naverages which confirmed the high performance of DeePiCt and enabled the classification of a subset \nof 60S large subunits (Supplementary Fig. 2). In comparison, most DEF ribosomes from ground truth \nannotations or DeePiCt predictions clustered in poorly defined classes and no 60S large subunits could \nbe separated even when classification results were improved by optimized particle poses (coordinates \nand orientations) after multi-particle refinement in M10 (Supplementary Fig. 4, 5). Also, in \nclassifications focused on an area close to the head of the ribosomal small subunit and at the peptide \nexit tunnel the additional head density was already apparent in 2D slices of the respective classes in the \nVPP data (Supplementary Fig. 6 and 7, respectively). Similarly, ribosomes close to ER and \nmitochondria that clustered into classes with adjacent membrane densities are more pronounced in VPP \n(Supplementary Fig. 8 and 9, respectively). \n  \nHowever, fine structural details that provide insights into functional configurations of molecular \ncomplexes are lost or cannot be recovered in the final reconstructions of VPP tomography data9. This \nhas been suggested to be caused by inaccurate weighing9 and a signal loss at high spatial frequencies11. \nThe preservation of high-resolution information in DEF in comparison to VPP becomes especially \napparent when looking at fine structural details in the subtomogram averages. Densities fitting ACPs of \nFAS (Extended data Fig. 8 d-e), P-site tRNAs (Extended data Fig. 8 j-k) inside the 80S ribosome \nand densities connecting ribosomes to an adjacent mitochondrion membrane (Supplementary Fig. 9) \ncould only be resolved in DEF data. \n  \nWe conclude that the usage of a VPP in tomography acquisition facilitated data mining including the \ndemonstrated domain generalization of applying DeePiCt models trained in VPP to DEF, and improved \n3D classifications. This makes it a valuable tool that ultimately provides benefits at the exploratory \nphase of structural analysis, helping the design of experiments and analysis pipelines that can eventually \nbe performed on DEF data from which higher resolution reconstructions can be obtained. \n', metadata={'x0': 72.0, 'x1': 526.2098999023438, 'y0': 53.400001525878906, 'y1': 84.70805358886719, 'width': 454.20989990234375, 'height': 593.9859619140625, 'nlines': 41, 'fs': 14.487462485708841, 'file_path': '/Users/gburns/Documents/2023H2/cryoet_portal_rocrates/reading/cryoet_papers/de_teresa-2022-10.1101-2022.04.12.488077.pdf', 'page': 65, 'total_pages': 66}),

from __future__ import annotations

from abc import abstractmethod
from typing import List
import re

from langchain.schema import BaseOutputParser, OutputParserException

import json
from json import JSONDecodeError
from typing import Any, List

class JsonEnclosedByTextOutputParser(BaseOutputParser[Any]):
    """Parse the output of an LLM call to a JSON object."""

    def parse(self, text: str) -> Any:
        text = text.strip()
        m = re.search('.*([\[\{](.|\n)*[\}\]]).*', text, flags=re.M)
        if m:
            text1 = m.group(1)
            try:
                return json.loads(text1)
            except JSONDecodeError as e:
                return None
                #raise OutputParserException(f"Invalid json output: {text1} derived from {text}") from e
        else: 
            raise OutputParserException(f"Could not find json-formatted data in: {text}")

    @property
    def _type(self) -> str:
        return "json_enclosed_by_text_output_parser"
from langchain import PromptTemplate
from langchain.output_parsers import CommaSeparatedListOutputParser

output_parser = JsonEnclosedByTextOutputParser()

t3 = """[INST]
<<SYS>>
Your job is to carefully read an excerpt from a scientific research article and perform the task described below. 
The text of the excerpt will be delimited with triple backticks. 

Be precise in your answers. 
<</SYS>>

1. Split the text of the excerpt into separate sentences.

2. Identify if there are any sentences in the excerpt that describe the process of annotating a dataset obtained using electron microsopy. 
If there no such sentences, return an empty list.

3. If there are any sentences that describe how annotations were obtained, write a short summary of the methods used. 
If there no such sentences, respond with "No methods described."

In addition, explain your reasoning briefly in one or two sentences.

excerpt: '''{excerpt}'''
[INST]
"""
